reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Triangular multiplicative update – incoming edges.
    Mirror of the outgoing variant: updates pair (i,j) by aggregating over
    the shared index k via edges (k,i) and (k,j).
    """

    def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden_dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.layer_norm_in = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.proj_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.layer_norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden_dim)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim)
        self.out_gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, pair_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_mask: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
            pair_mask: (B, N, N)
        Returns:
            updated pair representation (B, N, N, pair_dim)
        """
        mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)
        x = self.layer_norm_in(pair_repr)

        a = self.proj_a(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_a(x)) * mask  # (B, N, N, H)
        b = self.proj_b(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_b(x)) * mask

        # Incoming: aggregate over k with a[k,i] * b[k,j]
        out = torch.einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b)

        out = self.layer_norm_out(out)
        out = self.out_proj(out)
        out = torch.sigmoid(self.out_gate(pair_repr)) * out
        return out


pair_dim = 64
hidden_dim = 32
seq_len = 32
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    pair_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    pair_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len)
    return [pair_repr, pair_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [pair_dim, hidden_dim]

reasoning
We need to rewrite the forward computation as a custom CUDA kernel. The model is a triangular multiplicative update with incoming edges. Let's understand the computation: Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, pair_dim), pair_mask (B, N, N). 1. mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1) -> (B, N, N, 1) 2. x = layer_norm_in(pair_repr) -> (B, N, N, pair_dim) 3. a = proj_a(x) * sigmoid(gate_a(x)) * mask -> (B, N, N, hidden_dim) Here proj_a and gate_a are Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim). So a = (x @ W_a + b_a) * sigmoid(x @ W_gate_a + b_gate_a) * mask 4. Similarly b = proj_b(x) * sigmoid(gate_b(x)) * mask -> (B, N, N, hidden_dim) 5. out = einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b) This is sum over k of a[b,k,i,h] * b[b,k,j,h] -> out[b,i,j,h] So effectively for each batch b, i, j, hidden h: out = sum_k a[b,k,i,h] * b[b,k,j,h] This is a matrix multiplication: reshape a as (B, N, N, H) where indices are (b,k,i,h). If we fix b and h, a[:, :, :, h] is (N, N) with first index k, second i. b[:, :, :, h] is (N, N) with first index k, second j. Then einsum is A^T @ B where A = a[...,h] of shape (N, N) (k,i) and B = b[...,h] of shape (N, N) (k,j). So for each b,h: out[b, :, :, h] = a[b,:,:,h].transpose(0,1) @ b[b,:,:,h] Or equivalently, out[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a[b,k,i,h] * b[b,k,j,h]. This is a batched matrix multiply: for each b and h, it's (N,N) @ (N,N) but with a transposed. Specifically: a_reshaped: (B*H, N, N) where for each bh, matrix is a[bh] with rows k, cols i. b_reshaped: (B*H, N, N) where rows k, cols j. Then we want out_reshaped: (B*H, N, N) = a_reshaped.transpose(1,2) @ b_reshaped. Wait: a_reshaped (B*H, N, N) rows=k, cols=i. Transpose -> rows=i, cols=k. Multiply by b (rows=k, cols=j) gives (i,j). Yes: out(b,h) = a(b,h).t() @ b(b,h). So a is (N_k, N_i), b is (N_k, N_j). Then a^t is (N_i, N_k) times b (N_k, N_j) -> (N_i, N_j). So we can compute out via a batched GEMM on (B*H) matrices. 6. out = layer_norm_out(out) -> (B, N, N, hidden_dim) 7. out = out_proj(out) where out_proj is Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim) -> (B, N, N, pair_dim) 8. out = sigmoid(out_gate(pair_repr)) * out where out_gate is Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim) -> (B, N, N, pair_dim) 9. return out Important to note: pair_mask is (B,N,N) and multiplied after the gate for a and b. Could also be fused. Performance: The operations are many small linear layers and a batched einsum. Since seq_len is small (32), batch_size=2, hidden_dim=32, pair_dim=64. This is tiny. A custom kernel that fuses everything could be much faster by avoiding multiple kernel launches and memory accesses. We need to write a Python file with ModelNew. Use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline. Define a CUDA kernel that does all the work. Since the dimensions are small, we can have each thread compute one output element (B,N,N,pair_dim). But we need to perform reductions over k (N=32) and over pair_dim (64) for the linear layers. We need to be careful with numerical precision: fp32 atol=rtol=1e-4. So accumulation order may matter slightly but likely okay. Strategy: - In forward, compute x = layer_norm_in(pair_repr). We can do this in PyTorch or in CUDA. Since it's just LayerNorm over last dim (pair_dim=64), it might be okay to keep in PyTorch, but to minimize overhead we can fuse everything. - The model weights: we need to access layer_norm_in weight and bias, proj_a weight and bias, gate_a weight and bias, etc. Since ModelNew is constructed with same args, we need to match the reference's parameters. The reference does: x = layer_norm_in(pair_repr) a = proj_a(x) * sigmoid(gate_a(x)) * mask b = proj_b(x) * sigmoid(gate_b(x)) * mask out = torch.einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b) out = layer_norm_out(out) out = out_proj(out) out = sigmoid(out_gate(pair_repr)) * out That's many kernel launches. A single fused kernel that does all this could be much faster. Given the small sizes, the best approach is to fuse everything into one kernel, with each thread computing one (b,i,j,p). Let's analyze the arithmetic intensity per thread. Per thread (b,i,j,p): 1. Compute out_gate_p = sigmoid( sum_d pair_repr[b,i,j,d] * W_gate[p,d] + b_gate[p] ). 2. Compute main_branch_p: a. For each k in [0,N): i. Load pair_repr[b,k,i,d] for d=0..P-1. ii. Compute layer norm of this vector. iii. Compute a_k_h = (x @ W_proj_a^T + b_proj_a)[h] * sigmoid(x @ W_gate_a^T + b_gate_a)[h] * mask[b,k,i] iv. Load pair_repr[b,k,j,d] for d=0..P-1. v. Compute layer norm of this vector. vi. Compute b_k_h = (x @ W_proj_b^T + b_gate_b)[h] * sigmoid(x @ W_gate_b^T + b_gate_b)[h] * mask[b,k,j] vii. For each h, accumulate a_k_h * b_k_h into out_h. b. After k loop, we have out_h for h=0..H-1. c. Apply layer_norm_out to out_h. d. Compute out_proj_p = sum_h out_norm_h * W_out[p,h] + b_out[p]. e. final = out_gate_p * out_proj_p. This is a lot of work per thread: for each k, we need to compute two layer norms (each over 64 elements), four linear layers (64->32) (proj_a, gate_a, proj_b, gate_b), and then accumulate H=32 products. Then another linear 32->1 (out_proj for a single p), plus layer norm over H. Arithmetic: For each k: - LN: mean and var reduction over 64: about 2*64 ops. Then normalize 64 elements. - Four linears: each is matrix-vector mult 64x32 = ~2048 mul-adds. Four of them = ~8192. - Multiply and accumulate: 32 multiplies and adds. Total per k: ~8192 + 128 + 64 ~ 8400 FLOPs. For N=32: ~268,800 FLOPs. Then out_proj: 32 mul-adds. Total per thread: ~269k FLOPs. With 2048 blocks * 64 threads = 131072 threads. Actually total output elements = B*N*N*P = 2*32*32*64 = 131072. So if each thread computes one p, we have 131072 threads. Each does 269k FLOPs -> total FLOPs = 131072 * 269k ≈ 35 billion FLOPs. Wait that seems huge. Let's recalc: The operations are shared across threads. For each (b,i,j,p), we recompute a and b for all k and all h. But a[b,k,i,h] does NOT depend on j or p. So if each (b,i,j,p) thread recomputes a for all k and h, that's huge redundant work. In the reference, a and b are computed once per (b,k,i,h). Then einsum reuses them across j and h? Actually a is computed for all (b,k,i). Then einsum uses a for all j. So a is reused N times (for each j). b is reused N times (for each i). In our per-thread-per-p approach, we would recompute a and b for each j and p, leading to factor N*P more work. That's terrible. So we need to share computation. We need a tiling strategy where a and b are computed once and reused. The best way is to have each thread block compute all p for a fixed (b,i,j), but compute a and b for all k,h once per block and store in shared memory / registers. Since for a fixed (b,i,j), a depends only on (b,k,i) and b depends only on (b,k,j). So within a block, we can compute a for all k,h and b for all k,h collaboratively. Block (b,i,j): - We have 32 threads (or 64). Let's use 64 threads. - Shared memory: - pair_i[k][d] = pair_repr[b,k,i,d] for k=0..31, d=0..63. Size: 32*64 = 2048 floats. - pair_j[k][d] = pair_repr[b,k,j,d] for k=0..31, d=0..63. Size: 2048 floats. - a[k][h] = ... size 32*32 = 1024 floats. - b[k][h] = ... size 32*32 = 1024 floats. - out_h[h] = sum_k a[k][h] * b[k][h]. Size 32 floats. - Perhaps scratchpad for LN. How to compute a[k][h] collaboratively: For each k (0..31): - Load pair_i[k][d] into registers or shared memory. Since pair_i is already in SMEM, we can read it. - Compute layer_norm of pair_i[k][:] (64 elements). With 64 threads, we can compute LN quickly using warp reductions. - Then compute proj_a and gate_a. Each is Linear(P=64->H=32). With 64 threads, we can compute the output vector of size 32 by having each thread compute one or two output elements. - For proj_a: W_proj_a is (H,P) = (32,64). For output h, it's dot(x, W[h,:]). With 64 threads, we can have 32 threads each compute one h, using a loop over d. But each thread needs to read x[d] and W[h,d]. Since x is in SMEM (or registers if we load it), and W is in global memory (constant or global). With 32 threads active, each does 64 mul-adds. That's fine. - Similarly gate_a. - Then a[k][h] = proj_a_h * sigmoid(gate_a_h) * mask[b,k,i]. - We can write a[k][h] to SMEM. - Similarly for b[k][h] using pair_j[k][:]. Kernel signature: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* pair_repr, const float* pair_mask, const float* ln_in_w, const float* ln_in_b, const float* proj_a_w, const float* proj_a_b, const float* gate_a_w, const float* gate_a_b, const float* proj_b_w, const float* proj_b_b, const float* gate_b_w, const float* gate_b_b, const float* ln_out_w, const float* ln_out_b, const float* out_proj_w, const float* out_proj_b, const float* out_gate_w, const float* out_gate_b, float* output, int B, int N, int P, int H ) ``` We need to store weights in global memory. The weights are constant per call. We can pass pointers. Actually PyTorch default eps for LayerNorm is 1e-5. Let's think about the reduction for layer norm. For each vector of size P=64, we need sum and sum of squares. With 64 threads (the block size), we can do a warp reduction within a warp. Since 64 threads = 2 warps, we can reduce across warps using shared memory or shfl. Simpler: use `__shfl_down_sync` within each warp, then combine across warps via SMEM. But note: for computing layer norm of pair_i[k][:] (64 elements), all 64 threads in the block will participate. We can do: ```cpp float val = pair_i[k][threadIdx.x]; float sum = val; for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); if (threadIdx.x < 32) smem_sum[threadIdx.x] = sum; // wait, we need cross-warp reduction. ``` Better: use a struct for reduction. Since block size is 64, we can do: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { static __shared__ float shared[2]; // one per warp int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < 2) ? shared[threadIdx.x] : 0; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); return val; } ``` Then mean and variance can be computed with two reductions per vector. But we need to compute LN for each k sequentially. For each k, we load pair_i[k][threadIdx.x] (if threadIdx.x < P). Since P=64 and block size=64, each thread loads one element. SMEM partition: ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* pair_i = smem; // N * P float* pair_j = pair_i + N * P; // N * P float* a_vals = pair_j + N * P; // N * H float* b_vals = a_vals + N * H; // N * H float* out_h = b_vals + N * H; // H float* out_norm = out_h + H; // H float* pair_ij = out_norm + H; // P // maybe scratch for reductions float* scratch = pair_ij + P; // 2 (for warp reductions) ``` Total elements: N*P*2 + N*H*2 + H*2 + P + 2. For N=32, P=64, H=32: 2048+2048+1024+1024+32+32+64+2 = 6274 floats. *4 = 25096 bytes. Now, the kernel steps: 1. For out_gate: load pair_repr[b,i,j,p] into pair_ij[p]. pair_ij[p] = pair_repr[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + p]; // We need this for the final multiplication. // Also precompute out_gate linear. // Actually we can compute out_gate at the end. Compute layer norm of pair_i[k][*] (size P). - Each thread d loads x = pair_i[k*P + d]. - Compute sum_x = blockReduceSum(x). - Compute sum_x2 = blockReduceSum(x*x). - mean = sum_x / P; - var = sum_x2 / P - mean*mean; - inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); - x_norm = (x - mean) * inv_std * ln_in_w[d] + ln_in_b[d]; - Store x_norm back to pair_i[k*P + d] = x_norm; __syncthreads(); b. Here's a snippet: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val, float* scratch) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) scratch[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? scratch[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); // broadcast result // We can store final in scratch[0] and read if (threadIdx.x == 0) scratch[0] = val; __syncthreads(); return scratch[0]; } ``` But we need to be careful with the scratch shared memory. Wait, we can compute variance via: ``` float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / P; float diff = x - mean; float var = blockReduceSum(diff * diff, scratch) / P; ``` This requires two __syncthreads inside blockReduceSum. That's okay. But we need to make sure scratch memory is used correctly. Actually we can make a function: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm(float* smem_vec, int size, float weight, float bias, float* scratch) { // assume threadIdx.x < size float x = smem_vec[threadIdx.x]; float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / size; float diff = x - mean; float sq = diff * diff; float var_sum = blockReduceSum(sq, scratch); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / size + 1e-5f); smem_vec[threadIdx.x] = diff * inv_std * weight + bias; } ``` But wait: blockReduceSum uses scratch and __syncthreads internally. After it returns, all threads have the reduced value (via scratch[0]). But we must be careful: inside blockReduceSum, after writing scratch[0], we have a __syncthreads. Then all threads read scratch[0] and return. So it's fine. We need to ensure that when we call blockReduceSum for the first time, then compute diff, then call blockReduceSum again, the scratch[0] used to broadcast the first sum is overwritten. That's fine. Wait, in blockReduceSum we have: ``` if (threadIdx.x == 0) scratch[0] = val; __syncthreads(); return scratch[0]; ``` If we call it again, scratch[0] is overwritten with the new val. That's fine. But what about the intermediate step `if (lane == 0) scratch[wid] = val;`? That writes scratch[wid]. Since wid is 0 or 1, it writes scratch[0] or scratch[1]. If blockDim.x is 64, wid max 1. After the first call, scratch[0] and scratch[1] contain warp sums. Then we read scratch[threadIdx.x]. That's okay. However, there is a subtle bug: after the first blockReduceSum returns, scratch[0] = sum. Then we compute diff = x - mean. Then we call blockReduceSum again. In the second call, we write scratch[wid] = val (warp sum). Then we read scratch[threadIdx.x]. This overwrites scratch[0] and scratch[1]. But after reading, we reduce and write scratch[0] = final var_sum. Then all threads return scratch[0]. That's fine. One issue: `blockReduceSum` uses `__syncthreads` inside. But if some threads take a divergent path, it's okay because all threads in the block call the same function. Actually we only call layer_norm on threads with threadIdx.x < size. If block size is 64 and size is 64, all threads call it. If size is 32 (for out_h), then only threads 0..31 call it. In that case, we need to be careful: the `__syncthreads` inside blockReduceSum will hang if only some threads reach it. So we must ensure that all threads in the block participate, or we structure the code to avoid syncthreads in divergent regions. For layer_norm of out_h (size 32), only 32 threads will call blockReduceSum. The other 32 threads (32..63) will not. That will cause deadlock because of __syncthreads inside blockReduceSum. To avoid this, we can make blockReduceSum not use __syncthreads, or ensure all threads call it. Since block size is 64 > 32, we need a reduction that works for a subset of active threads. Now, let's write a device function for layer norm in shared memory: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm_shared(float* vec, int size, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* scratch) { float x = (threadIdx.x < size) ? vec[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / size; float diff = x - mean; float sq = diff * diff; float var_sum = blockReduceSum(sq, scratch); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / size + 1e-5f); if (threadIdx.x < size) { vec[threadIdx.x] = diff * inv_std * weight[threadIdx.x] + bias[threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, `weight` and `bias` are global pointers. Actually `__shfl_down_sync` with mask `0xffffffff` requires that all lanes in the warp are convergent (i.e., no divergence within the warp). In `if (wid == 0)`, for warp 0, all threads are active. The CUDA documentation says that __shfl_down_sync must be called by all threads in the warp that are specified by the mask. For fixed b,i, and varying k,d. If we linearize idx = k*P + d for the inner N*P elements. In our load loop, `idx = tid; idx < total_load; idx += blockDim.x`. The addressing is linear in idx, but `k = idx / P`, `d = idx % P`. So consecutive threads access consecutive `idx`, which means consecutive `k*P+d`. Are these consecutive in memory? Yes, because for a fixed (b,i), the slice `pair_repr[b, :, i, :]` is contiguous in memory? Let's see: pair_repr shape (B,N,N,P). stride: (N*N*P, N*P, P, 1). So for fixed b,i, the index is `offset = b*N*N*P + i*P + k*N*P + d`. Wait: index = ((b*N + k)*N + i)*P + d = b*N*N*P + k*N*P + i*P + d. So as k increments, the offset jumps by N*P. So the slice for fixed b,i is not contiguous; it's strided by N*P. The elements for consecutive k at same d are N*P apart. But within a given k, d increments by 1 are contiguous. Our load loop `idx = tid` gives `k = idx / P`, `d = idx % P`. So thread 0 accesses k=0,d=0. Thread 1 accesses k=0,d=1. These are contiguous (offset +1). Thread P-1 accesses k=0,d=P-1. Thread P accesses k=1,d=0. That's offset + N*P - (P-1). That's a stride of (N-1)*P+1 = 31*64+1 = 1985. That's not coalesced if consecutive threads access elements far apart. Actually, for pair_i, we want to load all (k,d). Since the memory layout for fixed b,i is `offset(b,k,i,d) = b*N*N*P + k*N*P + i*P + d`. So within a k, d is contiguous. But across k, it's strided by N*P. To get coalesced loads, it's better to have each k loaded by a warp or thread block with contiguous d. Since blockDim.x = P = 64, we can load one full k per block of threads, but threads are assigned by linear idx. Let's restructure: For each k, threads 0..P-1 load d=0..P-1. That's perfect coalescing: thread d loads `offset + k*N*P + d`. These are contiguous. So we should loop over k and have all threads load their d. Similarly for pair_j. So instead of the linearized loop over idx, we should do: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { int d = threadIdx.x; if (d < P) { pair_i[k * P + d] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + i) * P + d]; pair_j[k * P + d] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + j) * P + d]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` But note: for k=0..31, threadIdx.x=0..63 loads d. The memory offsets are `base + k*N*P + d`. For a given k, threads access contiguous memory. The address for thread d is `base + k*N*P + d`. Since `base` depends on i and j, it's different for pair_i and pair_j. So we can't load both simultaneously with the same threads without striding. But we can do them sequentially in the same loop: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { int d = threadIdx.x; pair_i[k * P + d] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + i) * P + d]; pair_j[k * P + d] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + j) * P + d]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, is `pair_repr[...]` coalesced? For pair_i, all threads read `base_i + k*N*P + d`. For a fixed k, these are contiguous addresses. So yes, coalesced. For pair_j, base_j is different, but still contiguous for each k. However, the two reads are to different addresses, which might cause some serialization but still coalesced. We also need to load pair_ij. That's just one vector: `pair_repr[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + d]`. Coalesced. This is much better than the linearized loop. Now, the total number of loads: For each k, 128 floats (64 for pair_i, 64 for pair_j). 32 iterations = 4096 floats. Each thread loads 2 floats per iteration, total 64 iterations. That's fine. Alternatively, we can load pair_i and pair_j in two separate loops to avoid instruction interleaving. Probably fine. We should also consider using `const float*` for inputs. And maybe `__restrict__` for pointer arguments. Might help compiler. Now, let's think about the out_proj and out_gate loops. For out_proj, each thread computes dot product over H=32. Now, let's write the C++ code. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val, float* scratch) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) scratch[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x >> 5)) ? scratch[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) scratch[0] = val; __syncthreads(); return scratch[0]; } __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm_shared(float* vec, int size, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* scratch) { float x = (threadIdx.x < size) ? vec[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / size; float diff = x - mean; float sq = diff * diff; float var_sum = blockReduceSum(sq, scratch); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / size + 1e-5f); if (threadIdx.x < size) { vec[threadIdx.x] = diff * inv_std * weight[threadIdx.x] + bias[threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); } __inline__ __device__ float sigmoidf(float x) { return (x >= 0.0f) ? (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) : (expf(x) / (1.0f + expf(x))); } __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_b, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int P, int H ) { extern __shared__ float smem_buf[]; // Layout float* row_i = smem_buf; // P float* row_j = row_i + P; // P float* a_vals = row_j + P; // N*H float* b_vals = a_vals + N * H; // N*H float* out_h = b_vals + N * H; // H float* out_norm = out_h + H; // H float* pair_ij = out_norm + H; // P float* scratch = pair_ij + P; // at least blockDim.x/32 int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] for out_gate if (tid < P) { pair_ij[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Loop over k to compute a_vals and b_vals for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { // Load row i and row j if (tid < P) { row_i[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + i) * P + tid]; row_j[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Layer norm row_i layer_norm_shared(row_i, P, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, scratch); // Compute a_vals for this k if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float acc_a = proj_a_b[h]; float acc_g = gate_a_b[h]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { float xv = row_i[d]; acc_a += xv * proj_a_w[h * P + d]; acc_g += xv * gate_a_w[h * P + d]; } float mask_val = pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + i]; a_vals[k * H + h] = acc_a * sigmoidf(acc_g) * mask_val; } __syncthreads(); // Layer norm row_j layer_norm_shared(row_j, P, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, scratch); // Compute b_vals for this k if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float acc_b = proj_b_b[h]; float acc_g = gate_b_b[h]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { float xv = row_j[d]; acc_b += xv * proj_b_w[h * P + d]; acc_g += xv * gate_b_w[h * P + d]; } float mask_val = pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + j]; b_vals[k * H + h] = acc_b * sigmoidf(acc_g) * mask_val; } __syncthreads(); } // Compute out_h[h] = sum_k a_vals[k,h] * b_vals[k,h] if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float acc = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { acc += a_vals[k * H + h] * b_vals[k * H + h]; } out_h[h] = acc; } __syncthreads(); // Layer norm out_h layer_norm_shared(out_h, H, ln_out_w, ln_out_b, scratch); // Compute out_proj for this thread's p = tid float out_p = out_proj_b[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { out_p += out_h[h] * out_proj_w[tid * H + h]; } // Compute out_gate on pair_ij float gate_acc = out_gate_b[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { gate_acc += pair_ij[d] * out_gate_w[tid * P + d]; } float sig_gate = sigmoidf(gate_acc); // Write output output[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + tid] = sig_gate * out_p; } torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b, torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b, torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b, torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b, torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b, torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b, torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b, torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b ) { int B = pair_repr.size(0); int N = pair_repr.size(1); int P = pair_repr.size(3); int H = proj_a_w.size(0); auto output = torch::empty_like(pair_repr); dim3 grid(B, N, N); dim3 block(P); int scratch_size = (P + 31) / 32; size_t smem_size = (P + P + N * H + N * H + H + H + P + scratch_size) * sizeof(float); fused_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(), pair_mask.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_b.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, P, H ); AT_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); return output; } ``` Now, Python code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; For loops with unknown upper bound (P, H from args), `#pragma unroll` might not work or might unroll partially. The compiler will likely ignore it if the bound is not constant. It's fine. Sigmoid stable implementation: Used `sigmoidf(x)`. PyTorch uses `1 / (1 + exp(-x))` with stable implementation that handles large negative/positive. Our version is also stable. 3. Mask multiplication: The reference does `a = ... * mask` where mask shape is `(B,N,N,1)`. In our kernel, `mask_val` is a scalar from `pair_mask`. We multiply `acc_a * sigmoid(...) * mask_val`. Correct. 4. Einsum: `out = torch.einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b)`. In kernel, `out_h[h] = sum_k a_vals[k*H+h] * b_vals[k*H+h]`. Note that a_vals is for fixed i, b_vals for fixed j. Flatten row-major: `h * P + d`. Good. Now, check if any weight needs transpose. In PyTorch, `y = x @ W^T + b`, where W is (out, in). So `y[h] = sum_d x[d] * W[h,d]`. But `wid` could be 0 or 1. So `scratch[0]` is overwritten. That's intended. But what if `scratch` is also being read by other threads? The __syncthreads inside blockReduceSum handles it. This __syncthreads ensures all threads finished reading `scratch[0]` from the first call. Because the first call ended with `__syncthreads(); return scratch[0];`. So 64 LNs and 128 linears per block. Each block writes 64 floats. This is compute-heavy enough to keep the SMs busy. With 2048 blocks and maybe 80 SMs, each SM gets ~25 blocks. But P=64. Not needed. Wait, block size is P=64. That's 2 warps. Occupancy is limited by shared memory: 2306*4 = 9224 bytes per block. On Ampere (max 164KB shared per SM, but max 48KB or 96KB per block depending on config). The default max shared memory per block is 48KB. So with ~9KB per block, we can have up to 5 blocks per SM (5*9=45KB). With 2 warps per block, that's 10 warps per SM, which is okay but not great. On A100, max warps per SM is 64. So 10 warps is ~15% occupancy. Might still be enough for compute-bound kernel. But is it compute-bound? Let's count FLOPs per block more accurately. For each k (32): - LN: sum reduction (64 adds), mean, diff (64 subs), sq (64 muls), sum reduction (64 adds), rsqrt, scale (64 muls, 64 adds). Total ~448 FLOPs per LN. 2 LNs per k = 896. - Linear proj_a: 64*32 = 2048 mul-adds. gate_a: 2048. proj_b: 2048. gate_b: 2048. Total 8192. - Sigmoids and multiplies: negligible. Total per k: ~9088 FLOPs. For N=32: ~290k FLOPs. Einsum: 32*32 = 1024 mul-adds. LN out: ~224 FLOPs (size 32). out_proj: 64*32 = 2048 mul-adds. out_gate: 64*64 = 4096 mul-adds. Total per block: ~300k FLOPs. With 2 warps per SM (10 warps total), each warp has 32 threads. So per warp: 150k FLOPs. At 1.5 GHz, a warp does ~1.5k FLOPs per cycle (with tensor cores) or ~64 per cycle (with FP32 units). Actually FP32 throughput on Ampere is 64 per SM per cycle? Wait, Ampere has 4 warp schedulers, each with 16 FP32 units, so 64 FP32 ops per cycle per SM. With 10 warps, we can saturate if there are enough independent instructions. But we have a lot of dependent loads. Hmm. Actually, this kernel is likely memory latency bound because of the many small loops and shared memory accesses. With only 2 warps per block, latency hiding might be poor. But the total number of blocks is high (2048), so maybe the GPU can switch between blocks on the same SM? With 5 blocks per SM, that's 10 warps. That's enough to hide some latency, but maybe not all. Could we improve occupancy by reducing SMEM usage further? We could remove `a_vals` and `b_vals` and accumulate the einsum on the fly? Let's think: For each k, we compute a_vals[k,:] and b_vals[k,:]. We could accumulate into out_h directly: For each k, after computing a and b, each thread h could do `out_h[h] += a_k[h] * b_k[h]`. But `a_k[h]` and `b_k[h]` are computed by threads h. After computing a_k and b_k, we could have each thread h do `out_h[h] += a_k[h] * b_k[h]`. Then we don't need to store all a_vals and b_vals! We just need a temporary `a_k[H]` and `b_k[H]` in SMEM (or registers for each thread). Since each thread h has its own a and b, it can keep them in registers. But then we need to synchronize after computing a_k and b_k before accumulating. Actually, we can accumulate in registers per thread: each thread h computes `acc[h] += a_k_h * b_k_h`. But thread h only computes one h. So we can have a register `float acc_h = 0;` for each thread (only one value). Then for each k, after computing a_k_h and b_k_h, we do `acc_h += a_k_h * b_k_h;`. That means we don't need `a_vals` and `b_vals` arrays at all! We only need to keep a running sum per h in registers (or SMEM if needed for later). This would reduce SMEM by N*H*2 = 8KB, bringing SMEM down to ~1KB (row_i, row_j, out_h, out_norm, pair_ij, scratch). That would drastically improve occupancy. But can we do `acc_h += a_k_h * b_k_h` without storing a and b? Let's see. In the current design, we compute a_k_h, write to SMEM, compute b_k_h, write to SMEM, then later sum over k. But we can compute a_k_h and b_k_h in the same loop over k and immediately multiply and accumulate into a register `acc_h`. However, there is a problem: `a_k_h` depends on row_i (pair_repr[b,k,i,:]) and `b_k_h` depends on row_j (pair_repr[b,k,j,:]). For a given k, we compute row_i, then a_k_h. Then row_j, then b_k_h. After both are ready, we can do `acc_h += a_k_h * b_k_h`. This requires that each thread holds `a_k_h` in a register while computing `b_k_h`. That's fine: thread h computes `a_k_h`, stores it in a register `a_reg`. Then computes `b_k_h`, stores in `b_reg`. Then `acc_h += a_reg * b_reg`. Then proceed to next k. We don't need `a_vals` and `b_vals` SMEM arrays! This is great. But wait: after the k-loop, we need `out_h[h] = acc_h`. We can write `out_h[h] = acc_h;` to SMEM. Since each thread has one h, we can use a register for `acc_h` and then write to SMEM. Let's redesign without a_vals and b_vals. SMEM layout: - row_i [P] - row_j [P] - out_h [H] - out_norm [H] (we might reuse out_h after LN) - pair_ij [P] - scratch [scratch_size] Total: P + P + H + H + P + scratch_size = 64+64+32+32+64+2 = 258 floats = ~1KB. That's amazing. Now, the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { extern __shared__ float smem_buf[]; float* row_i = smem_buf; float* row_j = row_i + P; float* out_h = row_j + P; float* pair_ij = out_h + H; float* scratch = pair_ij + P; int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid < P) { pair_ij[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); float acc_h = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { if (tid < P) { row_i[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + i) * P + tid]; row_j[tid] = pair_repr[(((b * N) + k) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); layer_norm_shared(row_i, P, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, scratch); float a_reg = 0.0f; if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float acc_a = proj_a_b[h]; float acc_g = gate_a_b[h]; for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { float xv = row_i[d]; acc_a += xv * proj_a_w[h * P + d]; acc_g += xv * gate_a_w[h * P + d]; } a_reg = acc_a * sigmoidf(acc_g) * pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + i]; } __syncthreads(); layer_norm_shared(row_j, P, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, scratch); float b_reg = 0.0f; if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float acc_b = proj_b_b[h]; float acc_g = gate_b_b[h]; for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { float xv = row_j[d]; acc_b += xv * proj_b_w[h * P + d]; acc_g += xv * gate_b_w[h * P + d]; } b_reg = acc_b * sigmoidf(acc_g) * pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + j]; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < H) { acc_h += a_reg * b_reg; } } if (tid < H) { out_h[tid] = acc_h; } __syncthreads(); layer_norm_shared(out_h, H, ln_out_w, ln_out_b, scratch); float out_p = out_proj_b[tid]; for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { out_p += out_h[h] * out_proj_w[tid * H + h]; } float gate_acc = out_gate_b[tid]; for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) { gate_acc += pair_ij[d] * out_gate_w[tid * P + d]; } float sig_gate = sigmoidf(gate_acc); output[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + tid] = sig_gate * out_p; } ``` This looks correct. We need to be careful that `sigmoidf` is defined before the kernel. After loading row_i, row_j: all reach. 2. Inside `layer_norm_shared(row_i)`: all reach multiple syncthreads (inside blockReduceSum and at the end). 3. After computing a_reg: all reach. 4. All threads call it. - Then compute `out_p`, `gate_acc`, etc. - Finally write output. No syncthreads needed after output write. One more check: `out_h` is size H. `layer_norm_shared` reads `vec[threadIdx.x]`. For tid >= H, it reads 0. So the reduction includes zeros. Since H is the exact size (32), and blockDim.x = P = 64, the extra zeros don't affect mean or variance (they just add 0 to sum and 0 to diff^2). So mean = sum_{h<H} out_h[h] / H. Var = sum_{h<H} (out_h[h]-mean)^2 / H. The zeros from tid>=H add (0-mean)^2 to var if not careful? Wait, `x = (tid < size) ? vec[tid] : 0.0f;`. For tid >= H, x = 0. diff = x - mean = -mean. sq = diff^2 = mean^2. blockReduceSum(sq) would include these extra mean^2 terms from the dummy threads, which is wrong! This is a critical bug! In the previous design with a_vals and b_vals, we didn't realize that `layer_norm_shared` with size < blockDim.x would include dummy zeros in the variance calculation, because `x = 0` for dummy threads, and `diff = -mean`, `sq = mean^2`. That would inflate variance. We need to fix `layer_norm_shared` to handle `size < blockDim.x` correctly. The reduction must be over only `size` elements. For `size < blockDim.x`, we should set `x = (tid < size) ? vec[tid] : 0.0f;` for sum, which is fine because the extra zeros don't change sum. But for variance, we need `diff = (tid < size) ? (vec[tid] - mean) : 0.0f;`. Then `sq = diff * diff`. That way, dummy threads contribute 0 to variance. So: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm_shared(float* vec, int size, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* scratch) { float x = (threadIdx.x < size) ? vec[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / size; float diff = (threadIdx.x < size) ? (vec[threadIdx.x] - mean) : 0.0f; float sq = diff * diff; float var_sum = blockReduceSum(sq, scratch); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / size + 1e-5f); if (threadIdx.x < size) { vec[threadIdx.x] = diff * inv_std * weight[threadIdx.x] + bias[threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Yes, that's the fix. For `size=64`, `threadIdx.x < size` is always true (since block size = P = 64). So no issue. For `size=32` (out_h), dummy threads (32..63) set `diff = 0`, so variance is correct. Similarly, for `size=64`, block size 64, all threads participate. Good. Let's update the code accordingly. Also, for `row_i` and `row_j`, size = P = blockDim.x. So `threadIdx.x < P` is always true. We can keep the `if (tid < P)` guards for generality. Now, with this fix, the kernel is correct. For warp 1 (threads 32-63), val=0. `scratch[1] = 0`. Then `val = (tid < 2) ? scratch[tid] : 0`. So second reduction gets sum of warp0 and warp1. Correct. So `layer_norm_shared` is now correct. Also, we need to make sure that the C++ string doesn't have any invalid characters. In Python triple-quoted string, backslashes are okay, but if we have `\n` in C++, it's just newline. `%` is okay. `"""` is not present. One subtle compile bug: `blockReduceSum` uses `__syncthreads()` inside, and it's called from `layer_norm_shared`, which also has `__syncthreads()` at the end. When we call `layer_norm_shared(row_i, ...)`, then `__syncthreads()`, then `layer_norm_shared(row_j, ...)`, then `__syncthreads()`, it's fine. But there is a known issue: `__syncthreads()` cannot be used in conditional branches where not all threads in the block take the same path. In our code, `layer_norm_shared` is called unconditionally by all threads. Inside `layer_norm_shared`, `blockReduceSum` is called unconditionally. In `blockReduceSum`, the only conditional is `if (lane == 0)` and `if (wid == 0)` and `if (threadIdx.x == 0)`. All of these are warp-uniform or followed by `__syncthreads()` at the end of the function. So it's safe. No divergence. `if (wid == 0)` is warp-uniform for each warp: warp 0 true, warp 1 false. But `__syncthreads()` after it is outside the if, executed by all threads. This is allowed in CUDA: `__syncthreads()` can be used after a branch if all threads in the block reach the `__syncthreads()` regardless of the branch. Here, threads in warp 1 skip `warpReduceSum` but still execute `__syncthreads()`. Good. `if (threadIdx.x == 0)` followed by `__syncthreads()` - all threads execute the syncthreads. Good. Let's write it. ```python cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val, float* scratch) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) scratch[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x >> 5)) ? scratch[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) scratch[0] = val; __syncthreads(); return scratch[0]; } __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm_shared(float* vec, int size, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* scratch) { float x = (threadIdx.x < size) ? vec[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; float sum = blockReduceSum(x, scratch); float mean = sum / size; float diff = (threadIdx.x < size) ? (vec[threadIdx.x] - mean) : 0.0f; float sq = diff * diff; float var_sum = blockReduceSum(sq, scratch); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / size + 1e-5f); if (threadIdx.x < size) { vec[threadIdx.x] = diff * inv_std * weight[threadIdx.x] + bias[threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); } __inline__ __device__ float sigmoidf(float x) { return (x >= 0.0f) ? int N = pair_repr.size(1); int P = pair_repr.size(3); int H = proj_a_w.size(0); auto output = torch::empty_like(pair_repr); dim3 grid(B, N, N); dim3 block(P); int scratch_size = (P + 31) / 32; size_t smem_size = (P + P + H + P + scratch_size) * sizeof(float); fused_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(), pair_mask.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_b.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, P, H ); = proj_a_b[h];\n float acc_g = gate_a_b[h];\n for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) {\n float xv = row_i[d];\n acc_a += xv * proj_a_w[h * P + d];\n acc_g += xv * gate_a_w[h * P + d];\n }\n a_reg = acc_a * sigmoidf(acc_g) * pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n layer_norm_shared(row_j, P, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, scratch);\n \n float b_reg = 0.0f;\n if (tid < H) {\n int h = tid;\n float acc_b = proj_b_b[h];\n float acc_g = gate_b_b[h];\n for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) {\n float xv = row_j[d];\n acc_b += xv * proj_b_w[h * P + d];\n acc_g += xv * gate_b_w[h * P + d];\n }\n b_reg = acc_b * sigmoidf(acc_g) * pair_mask[((b * N) + k) * N + j];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n if (tid < H) {\n acc_h += a_reg * b_reg;\n }\n }\n \n if (tid < H) {\n out_h[tid] = acc_h;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n layer_norm_shared(out_h, H, ln_out_w, ln_out_b, scratch);\n \n float out_p = out_proj_b[tid];\n for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) {\n out_p += out_h[h] * out_proj_w[tid * H + h];\n }\n \n float gate_acc = out_gate_b[tid];\n for (int d = 0; d < P; ++d) {\n gate_acc += pair_ij[d] * out_gate_w[tid * P + d];\n }\n float sig_gate = sigmoidf(gate_acc);\n \n output[(((b * N) + i) * N + j) * P + tid] = reasoning
Let me fix the CUDA code. The issue is `AT_CUDA_CHECK` - I should use `C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK()` instead. Also, I need to rethink the kernel design significantly. The current kernel design has issues: 1. `AT_CUDA_CHECK` macro not found - use `C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK()` 2. The kernel uses a block per (b, i, j) with P threads. But P=64, which means we have 2 warps but only some threads doing work for the hidden dimension H=32. 3. For H=32, only half the threads are doing useful work in the einsum part. 4. The layer norm is being done redundantly for each k loop iteration. Let me completely redesign this kernel more carefully: **Key insight**: The einsum `bkih,bkjh->bijh` can be computed as a matrix multiplication. If we reshape: - a: (B, N, N, H) -> (B*N, N, H) where we index by (b, i, k) for a - Actually reshaping: a[b, k, i, :] and b[b, k, j, :] - so for fixed b, i, j, we dot-product over k. For each (b, i, j), we compute sum_k a[b,k,i,:] * b[b,k,j,:]. This is essentially a batched GEMM where for each (b, i, j), the vectors a[b,:,i,:] and b[b,:,j,:] are dotted over the k dimension. For small N=32, a direct CUDA kernel is feasible, but we need to be smart about it. **New design approach:** 1. First, in a separate kernel or using a PyTorch ops, compute: - `x = layer_norm(pair_repr)` - `a = proj_a(x) * sigmoid(gate_a(x)) * mask` - `b = proj_b(x) * sigmoid(gate_b(x)) * mask` 2. Then compute the einsum `bkih,bkjh->bijh` using a custom kernel or cuBLAS batched GEMM. 3. Then apply layer_norm_out, out_proj, and the output gate. Actually, for the einsum, we can view it as: - For each (b, h), we want C[b, :, :, h] = A[b, :, :, h] @ B[b, :, :, h].T where A[b, k, i, h] and B[b, k, j, h] This is a batched matrix multiplication over (b, h) dimensions with matrix sizes (N, N) @ (N, N).T -> (N, N) Wait, A is (B, N, N, H) and B is (B, N, N, H). For each b and h: - A_slice = A[b, :, :, h] is (N, N) - B_slice = B[b, :, :, h] is (N, N) - C_slice = A_slice @ B_slice.T is (N, N) Actually: `out[b, i, j, h] = sum_k a[b, k, i, h] * b[b, k, j, h]` This is: C[b, h, i, j] = sum_k A[b, h, i, k] * B[b, h, j, k] Where A[b, h, i, k] = a[b, k, i, h] and B[b, h, j, k] = b[b, k, j, h] So C = A @ B.T where A and B are reshaped to (B*H, N, N) This is exactly `torch.bmm(A, B.transpose(-1, -2))` for tensors of shape (B*H, N, N). So we can do: 1. Compute a and b with fused kernel 2. Reshape and use `torch.bmm` for the einsum 3. Apply the rest This should be very fast since bmm uses cuBLAS. Actually, even simpler: `out = torch.einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b)` in PyTorch likely gets optimized to a batched GEMM. But since we're writing a custom kernel, let me try using bmm explicitly. Let me write a cleaner two-kernel approach: Kernel 1: Fused preprocessing kernel - Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, P), pair_mask (B, N, N) - Output: a (B, N, N, H), b (B, N, N, H) - For each (b, n1, n2), compute: - layer_norm_in on pair_repr[b, n1, n2, :] - a = proj_a(x) * sigmoid(gate_a(x)) * mask - b = proj_b(x) * sigmoid(gate_b(x)) * mask Kernel 2: Fused postprocessing kernel - Input: einsum_out (B, N, N, H), pair_repr_orig (B, N, N, P) - Output: final (B, N, N, P) - For each (b, i, j), compute: - layer_norm_out on einsum_out[b, i, j, :] - out_proj - sigmoid(out_gate(pair_repr_orig[b, i, j, :])) - multiply And the einsum in between can be done with `torch.bmm`. Actually, wait. Let me verify: einsum("bkih,bkjh->bijh", a, b) This is: for each b, i, j, h: sum over k of a[b,k,i,h] * b[b,k,j,h] If we reshape: a -> (B, N, N, H), permute to (B, H, N, N) -> (B*H, N, N) Then b -> (B, H, N, N) -> (B*H, N, N) out[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a_permuted[b,h,i,k] * b_permuted[b,h,j,k] So out_permuted[b,h,i,j] = sum_k A[b,h,i,k] * B[b,h,j,k] This is A @ B^T where A and B are (B*H, N, N). So: `out = torch.bmm(a_reshaped, b_reshaped.transpose(-1, -2))` where a_reshaped = a.permute(0,3,1,2).reshape(B*H, N, N) and b_reshaped = b.permute(0,3,1,2).reshape(B*H, N, N) Let me verify with a small example: a = tensor([[[[1,2],[3,4]], [[5,6],[7,8]]]]) # B=1, N=2, K=2, H=2 Actually let me use b,k,i,h indexing: a[b=0, k=0, i=0, h=0] = 1 a[b=0, k=0, i=0, h=1] = 2 a[b=0, k=0, i=1, h=0] = 3 a[b=0, k=0, i=1, h=1] = 4 a[b=0, k=1, i=0, h=0] = 5 a[b=0, k=1, i=0, h=1] = 6 a[b=0, k=1, i=1, h=0] = 7 a[b=0, k=1, i=1, h=1] = 8 b: same values out[b=0, i=0, j=0, h=0] = a[0,0,0,0]*b[0,0,0,0] + a[0,1,0,0]*b[0,1,0,0] = 1*1 + 5*5 = 26 out[b=0, i=0, j=1, h=0] = a[0,0,0,0]*b[0,0,1,0] + a[0,1,0,0]*b[0,1,1,0] = 1*3 + 5*7 = 38 out[b=0, i=1, j=0, h=0] = a[0,0,1,0]*b[0,0,0,0] + a[0,1,1,0]*b[0,1,0,0] = 3*1 + 7*5 = 38 out[b=0, i=1, j=1, h=0] = a[0,0,1,0]*b[0,0,1,0] + a[0,1,1,0]*b[0,1,1,0] = 3*3 + 7*7 = 58 A (after permute/reshape for h=0): [[1, 3], [5, 7]] B (after permute/reshape for h=0): [[1, 3], [5, 7]] A @ B^T = [[1+25, 3+35], [3+35, 9+49]] = [[26, 38], [38, 58]] Yes! It matches. OK, so the plan is: 1. Use a CUDA kernel to compute `a` and `b` from pair_repr with all the projections, gates, and masking 2. Use `torch.bmm` for the einsum 3. Use a CUDA kernel for the output projection and gating This should be quite fast. Let me also fuse the output bias into the matmul for better performance. Actually, for even more speedup, I could do everything in a single kernel. Let me think about whether it's worth it for N=32. For N=32, the einsum involves 32 multiplications and 31 additions per output element. For each (b, i, j, h), we have 32 FMAs. With B=2, N=32, H=32, that's 2*32*32*32 = 65536 output elements, each requiring 32 FMAs = ~2M FMAs. This is tiny. The bottleneck is probably memory bandwidth for reading pair_repr and writing intermediate tensors. If I use two kernels + bmm: - Kernel 1 reads pair_repr once, writes a and b: 2 * 64 * 4 bytes read + 2 * 32 * 4 bytes written per element = ~768 bytes per (b,n,n) element - For B*N*N = 2048 elements, total memory = ~1.5MB read + ~0.5MB written - bmm reads a and b, writes out: similar memory - Kernel 2 reads out and pair_repr, writes final: similar memory So total memory traffic is ~4-5MB, which at 3.35 TB/s would take ~1.5 microseconds. The compute is negligible. Detailed design: ``` extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* row_buffer = smem; // Size N*P for storing rows from global mem float* a_local = row_buffer + N*P; // Size N*H float* b_local = a_local + N*H; // Size N*H float* out_h = b_local + N*H; // Size H float* pair_ij = out_h + H; // Size P float* scratch = pair_ij + P; // Size 2 ``` Total shared memory: N*P + N*H + N*H + H + P + 2 = 32*64 + 32*32 + 32*32 + 32 + 64 + 2 = 2048 + 1024 + 1024 + 98 = 4194 floats ≈ 16.8KB. This fits easily in shared memory. Algorithm per block (b, i, j): 1. All 64 threads load pair_repr[b, k, i, :] for all k: - For k = 0 to 31, for each tid, load pair_repr[b, k, i, tid] if tid < 64, or pair_repr[b, k, i, tid-64] if tid >= 64... wait that's overloading. - Actually with 64 threads: for each k, thread tid loads pair_repr[b, k, i, tid] if tid < P. So for each k, we need 64 threads to load 64 elements. Over 32 k's, each thread loads 32 elements. This is: ``` for (int k = 0; k < N; k++) { if (tid < P) row_buffer[k*P + tid] = pair_repr[((b*N + k)*N + i)*P + tid]; } ``` But this is serialized over k with a syncthreads each time, which is slow. Better: let each thread load multiple elements: ``` for (int idx = tid; idx < N*P; idx += blockDim.x) { int k = idx / P; int d = idx % P; row_buffer[idx] = pair_repr[((b*N + k)*N + i)*P + d]; } ``` Similarly for pair_repr[b, k, j, :]. Compute a_kh for all h 4. Thread tid loads pair_repr[b, k, j, tid] if tid < P 5. Do layer norm on the row 6. Compute b_kh for all h 7. Accumulate a_kh * b_kh into out_h[h] For step 3 and 6, computing a_kh for all h requires each thread to compute one h, but that means reading the normalized row from shared memory. With H=32 and 64 threads, I can have 32 threads compute a and 32 compute b. But then I need to store both results. Wait, I only need the product a_kh * b_kh summed over k. Thread tid (if tid < H) computes b_k, tid by dot-product over P 5. Accumulate a_k,tid * b_k,tid into a register This avoids storing intermediate a and b values in shared memory, but each thread does 2*P FMAs per k (for the projections) and then multiplies. For N=32 k's, that's 2*32*64 = 4096 FMAs per thread for the projections, plus 32 multiplies. Total ~4128 FMAs per thread. With 32 threads, that's ~132K FMAs per block. At the same time, the block reads 2*N*P = 4096 floats from global memory (the pair_repr rows). This is actually compute-bound! 132K FMAs vs 4096 floats read. The compute intensity is ~32 FMAs per float read, which is great for GPU. Wait, but each thread is doing 4096 FMAs, and with 32 threads per block, that's 131K FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS (FP32 on H100), that's 131K / 67T = ~2 nanoseconds. But memory is 3.35 TB/s, so reading 4096*4 bytes = 16KB takes 16KB/3.35TB = ~5 nanoseconds. So memory is the bottleneck. But if data is in L2 cache (which it should be for N=32, B=2), the effective bandwidth is higher. And with many blocks running concurrently, we can hide latency. Let me go ahead and implement this design: ``` __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* pair_repr, const float* pair_mask, float* output, // all weights and biases... int B, int N, int P, int H ) { __shared__ float smem_row[P]; // For layer norm, one row at a time __shared__ float scratch[2]; // For block reduction __shared__ float pair_ij[P]; // Original pair_repr[b,i,j,:] for output gate int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] for the output gate if (tid < P) pair_ij[tid] = pair_repr[(((b*N + i)*N + j)*P + tid)]; __syncthreads(); float acc_h = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < N; k++) { // Load row_i = pair_repr[b, k, i, :] if (tid < P) smem_row[tid] = pair_repr[(((b*N + k)*N + i)*P + tid)]; __syncthreads(); // Layer norm on row_i ... float a_val = 0.0f; if (tid < H) { // Compute a projection and gate float proj = proj_a_b[tid]; float gate = gate_a_b[tid]; for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) { float x = smem_row[d]; proj += x * proj_a_w[tid*P + d]; gate += x * gate_a_w[tid*P + d]; } a_val = proj * sigmoid(gate) * pair_mask[((b*N + k)*N + i)]; } __syncthreads(); // Load row_j = pair_repr[b, k, j, :] if (tid < P) smem_row[tid] = pair_repr[(((b*N + k)*N + j)*P + tid)]; __syncthreads(); // Layer norm on row_j ... float b_val = 0.0f; if (tid < H) { float proj = proj_b_b[tid]; float gate = gate_b_b[tid]; for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) { float x = smem_row[d]; proj += x * proj_b_w[tid*P + d]; gate += x * gate_b_w[tid*P + d]; } b_val = proj * sigmoid(gate) * pair_mask[((b*N + k)*N + j)]; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < H) acc_h += a_val * b_val; } // Now apply output layer norm, projection, and gate // Layer norm on acc_h values in shared memory (H elements) if (tid < H) smem_row[tid] = acc_h; __syncthreads(); // layer_norm on H elements... // out_proj: P outputs from H inputs float out_val = out_proj_b[tid]; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) out_val += smem_row[h] * out_proj_w[tid*H + h]; // Output gate from pair_ij float gate_val = out_gate_b[tid]; for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) gate_val += pair_ij[d] * out_gate_w[tid*P + d]; output[(((b*N + i)*N + j)*P + tid)] = sigmoid(gate_val) * out_val; } ``` The issue with this design is: 1. For each k, we're doing layer norm twice. Layer norm requires block-level reductions which are somewhat expensive. 2. The weights are read from global memory for each k iteration. We could cache them in shared memory or rely on L1/L2 cache. For H=32, P=64, the weights for proj_a are 32*64 = 2048 floats = 8KB. If all blocks read these weights, they should stay in L2 cache. Actually, with 2048 blocks, each reading the same weights, the L1 cache per SM should help, but it's a lot of memory traffic. To optimize further, I could store the weight matrices in shared memory, but 4 weight matrices of 8KB each = 32KB, which is too much. I can store one at a time, but I'd need to reload for each k. Actually, each block only needs a subset of the weights. If tid handles one h, it only needs one row of each weight matrix (P floats). So each thread naturally caches its own row in registers, not shared memory. The dot-product loops over P, reading each weight matrix element once. With 32 iterations and good instruction-level parallelism, the weights should be pipelineable. One optimization: prefetch the next weight while computing the current one. The compiler might already do this. Another concern: the layer norm function I wrote uses `blockReduceSum` with `__syncthreads()` calls. In the loop over k, this adds synchronization overhead. Let me simplify the layer norm to use warp-level reductions only, since P=64 which is exactly 2 warps. ``` __inline__ __device__ void layer_norm_warp(float* data, int size, const float* w, const float* b) { // size = 64, 2 warps float x = data[threadIdx.x]; float sum = warpReduceSum(x); if (threadIdx.x == 0 || threadIdx.x == 32) { // combine warp sums } } ``` Actually, with 64 threads and 2 warps, I can do: ``` float sum = warpReduceSum(x); __shared__ float warp_sums[2]; if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = sum; __syncthreads(); float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]; mean = total / 64; ``` Then broadcast mean to all threads. Similarly for variance. This is more efficient than my previous blockReduceSum. OK let me write this properly now. Let me write this version. Kernel 1 (preprocess): - Grid: (B, N, N) - Block: 64 threads - For each (b, i, j): - Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] into smem[64] - Layer norm - Thread tid < 32: - Compute a[b, i, j, tid] and b[b, i, j, tid] - Write to global memory Kernel 3 (postprocess): - Grid: (B, N, N) - Block: 64 threads - For each (b, i, j): - Load einsum_out[b, i, j, :] into smem[32] - Layer norm on H=32 - Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] into registers - Each thread tid computes: - out_proj[tid] = bias + sum_h smem[h] * weight[tid, h] - gate = sigmoid(bias + sum_d pair_repr[d] * weight[tid, d]) - output = gate * out_proj This should be straightforward and fast enough. Let me estimate performance: - Kernel 1: reads 512KB (pair_repr), writes 256KB (a) + 256KB (b) = ~1MB traffic, compute is small - bmm: reads 512KB (a) + 512KB (b), writes 256KB (c) = ~1.3MB traffic - Kernel 3: reads 256KB (c) + 512KB (pair_repr), writes 512KB (output) = ~1.3MB traffic - Total: ~3.6MB - At 3.35 TB/s: ~1.1 microseconds (theoretical min) - Realistically with overheads: maybe 10-50 microseconds The PyTorch reference with separate ops likely takes 50-100 microseconds due to kernel launch overheads and multiple memory roundtrips. Our fused approach should be faster. Let me code this up now. One more thing: I'll write the kernels to accept transposed weight matrices. PyTorch Linear layers store weights as (out_features, in_features). In CUDA, for the dot-product `sum_d x[d] * w[h, d]`, it's better to have w in row-major with contiguous rows. PyTorch stores nn.Linear weights as (out_features, in_features) which is already row-major for each output feature. So `w[h*in_features + d]` is the correct access pattern. Actually wait, nn.Linear in PyTorch does `x @ W^T + b`, where W is (out_features, in_features). So for computing manually: `out[h] = sum_d x[d] * W[h, d] + b[h]`. Yes, `W[h*in_features + d]` is correct. Let me verify: `self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)`. Weight shape is (hidden_dim, pair_dim). Stored as (H, P). Accessing `proj_a_w[h*P + d]` gives W[h, d]. In the linear layer operation: `x @ W^T`. Wait, `nn.Linear` computes `y = xA^T + b`, where A is (out_features, in_features). So the weight matrix is A, and the computation is `sum_j x[j] * A[i, j]`. So yes, `w[h*P + d]` = A[h, d]. My manual dot-product `sum_d x[d] * w[h*P + d]` matches PyTorch exactly. OK let me write the code now. For gate computation: `gate_a(x) = x @ gate_a_W^T + gate_a_b`. And `sigmoid(gate_a(x))`. Then `proj_a(x) * sigmoid(gate_a(x))`. Wait, no. The original code is: `a = self.proj_a(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_a(x)) * mask` So first compute the projection, then compute the gate, then multiply elementwise. Yes, my code does this correctly. One more optimization: Instead of computing two separate dot products (one for proj, one for gate) over the same input x, I can interleave them or compute them together. But the compiler can handle this. Actually, for better instruction-level parallelism, I can accumulate both proj and gate in the same loop: ```cpp float proj = proj_b[h]; float gate = gate_b[h]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) { float xv = row[d]; proj += xv * proj_w[h*P + d]; gate += xv * gate_w[h*P + d]; } ``` This is what I'll write. Let me also think about whether to declare weights as `const float* __restrict__` for better cache behavior. OK, here's the complete code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __inline__ __device__ float sigmoidf(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } // Launch with blockDim.x = max(P, H) threads __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b, float* __restrict__ a_out, float* __restrict__ b_out, int B, int N, int P, int H ) { __shared__ float row[P]; __shared__ float warp_sums[2]; int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] into shared memory if (tid < P) { row[tid] = pair_repr[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Layer norm over P elements with blockDim.x threads // For P=64 and blockDim.x=64: each thread handles 1 element float x = (tid < P) ? row[tid] : 0.0f; // Warp reduce sum float sum = x; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) { warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum; // tid >> 5 is warp id (0 or 1) } __syncthreads(); float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]; float mean = total / P; float diff = (tid < P) ? (row[tid] - mean) : 0.0f; // Variance float sq = diff * diff; sum = sq; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum; __syncthreads(); float var = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]) / P; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); if (tid < P) { row[tid] = diff * inv_std * ln_w[tid] + ln_b[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute a and b projections if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float a_proj = proj_a_b[h]; float a_gate = gate_a_b[h]; float b_proj = proj_b_b[h]; float b_gate = gate_b_b[h]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) { float xv = row[d]; a_proj += xv * proj_a_w[h * P + d]; a_gate += xv * gate_a_w[h * P + d]; b_proj += xv * proj_b_w[h * P + d]; b_gate += xv * gate_b_w[h * P + d]; } float mask = pair_mask[(b * N + i) * N + j]; a_out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * H + h] = a_proj * sigmoidf(a_gate) * mask; b_out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * H + h] = b_proj * sigmoidf(b_gate) * mask; } } ``` And the postprocess kernel: ```cpp __global__ void postprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ einsum_out, const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int H, int P ) { __shared__ float einsum_row[32]; // H __shared__ float pair_row[64]; // P __shared__ float warp_sums[2]; int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load einsum result if (tid < H) { einsum_row[tid] = einsum_out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * H + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Layer norm on H elements float x = (tid < H) ? einsum_row[tid] : 0.0f; float sum = x; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum; __syncthreads(); float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]; float mean = total / H; float diff = (tid < H) ? (einsum_row[tid] - mean) : 0.0f; float sq = diff * diff; sum = sq; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum; __syncthreads(); float var = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]) / H; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); if (tid < H) { einsum_row[tid] = diff * inv_std * ln_w[tid] + ln_b[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Load original pair_repr for gate if (tid < P) { pair_row[tid] = pair_repr[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes one output element if (tid < P) { float out_val = out_proj_b[tid]; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { out_val += einsum_row[h] * out_proj_w[tid * H + h]; } float gate = out_gate_b[tid]; for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) { gate += pair_row[d] * out_gate_w[tid * P + d]; } output[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + tid] = sigmoidf(gate) * out_val; } } ``` Wait, I need to handle the case where H and P might have different values. For H=32 and blockDim.x = max(H, P) = 64: - In the layer norm for H=32, only threads 0-31 participate. - The warp reduction within threads 0-31 works fine. - `warp_sums[0]` gets the sum from warp 0. `warp_sums[1]` gets the sum from warp 1, but warp 1 has all zeros (since threads 32-63 evaluate x=0). - So total = warp_sums[0] + 0 = correct sum. - Same for variance. - This works! But I need to be careful: `warp_sums[1] = sum` for warp 1. Threads 32-63 have x=0, sum=0, so warp_sums[1] = 0. This is correct. Similarly for writes to einsum_row: only threads 0-31 write. Threads 32-63 don't write, which is fine. For the output: all 64 threads (tid < P=64) compute one output each. This design should work. Let me make sure the shared memory layout is correct. Actually, I realize `warp_sums` should be declared properly: ```cpp __shared__ float warp_sums[2]; ``` For 2 warps. In the layer norm, each warp's lane 0 writes to warp_sums[warp_id]. Wait, there's a potential race condition. Two warps might try to write to warp_sums simultaneously? No, because warp_sums[0] is only written by lane 0 of warp 0, and warp_sums[1] is only written by lane 0 of warp 1. These are different memory locations, so no race. But what if blockDim.x is not exactly 64? If I launch with 64 threads, there are exactly 2 warps. Good. What if H or P is not 32/64? In the PyTorch wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, ... ) { int B = pair_repr.size(0); int N = pair_repr.size(1); int P = pair_repr.size(3); int H = proj_a_w.size(0); auto a = torch::empty({B, N, N, H}, pair_repr.options()); auto b = torch::empty({B, N, N, H}, pair_repr.options()); dim3 grid(B, N, N); dim3 block(64); preprocess_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...); // Einsum via bmm auto a_r = a.permute({0, 3, 1, 2}).reshape({B * H, N, N}); auto b_r = b.permute({0, 3, 1, 2}).reshape({B * H, N, N}); auto c = torch::bmm(a_r, b_r.transpose(-1, -2)); auto einsum_out = c.reshape({B, H, N, N}).permute({0, 2, 3, 1}); auto output = torch::empty_like(pair_repr); postprocess_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(einsum_out, pair_repr, ..., output, B, N, H, P); return output; } ``` One concern: the permute and reshape operations create views, which are cheap. Wait, PyTorch uses row-major tensors by default, but cuBLAS expects column-major. PyTorch handles this by transposing the operation. For `torch.bmm(X, Y)`: - X has shape (batch, m, k) - Y has shape (batch, k, n) - Output has shape (batch, m, n) The actual cuBLAS call effectively computes C = A @ B where A and B are treated as column-major. For row-major input X of shape (batch, m, k), PyTorch passes it as the transpose to cuBLAS, so cuBLAS computes `Y^T @ X^T`, then transposes the result. This is getting complicated. Let me just test it or trust that PyTorch handles it correctly. Actually, I realize there might be a simpler approach. Since N=32, H=32, I could write a custom CUDA kernel for the einsum too. This avoids the bmm complexity entirely. For each (b, i, j, h) block of 32 threads, each thread reads a[b, k, i, h] and b[b, k, j, h] for its k, multiplies, and does warp reduce. Then writes the result. This is very simple and avoids the layout issues with bmm. Let me add this as an option. Custom einsum kernel: ```cpp __global__ void einsum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ a, const float* __restrict__ b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int H ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.w; int tid = threadIdx.x; float val = 0.0f; if (tid < N) { int k = tid; val = a[((b * H + h) * N + k) * N + i] * b[((b * H + h) * N + k) * N + j]; } // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } if (tid == 0) { out[((b * H + h) * N + i) * N + j] = val; } } ``` Wait, blockIdx.w doesn't exist in CUDA. The maximum grid dimension is 3D. So I'd need to combine dimensions. I can use: - grid = (B, N*N, H) where blockIdx.y = i*N + j - Or grid = (B, N, N) with H threads per block With H=32 threads per block and grid = (B, N, N): Each block computes all H outputs for one (b, i, j). Each thread h reads a[b, k, i, h] and b[b, k, j, h] for all k... that's a lot of reads. Better: grid = (B*H, N, N) with N threads per block. Each block computes one (b, h, i, j) output. Threads 0..N-1 each read one k and multiply. Warp reduce for sum. But N=32 threads per block, one warp. Grid = B*H*N*N = 2*32*32*32 = 65536 blocks. Each with 32 threads. Total memory reads: 65536 * 32 * 2 * 4 bytes = ~16MB. That's more than the input tensor itself because each a and b element is read multiple times. This is less efficient than bmm which would read each element once (with good caching). So using bmm is still better for the einsum. But to avoid the transpose layout complexity, I could write a and b in a layout that's naturally compatible with bmm. In my kernel, I access `proj_a_w[h * P + d]` which assumes row-major layout with H rows and P columns. nn.Linear weight is exactly this. So no transpose is needed. Let me write the complete code now.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = r"""
torch::Tensor forward_cuda(
    torch::Tensor pair_repr,
    torch::Tensor pair_mask,
    torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b,
    torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b,
    torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b,
    torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b,
    torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b,
    torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b,
    torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b,
    torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b
);
"""

cuda_source = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__inline__ __device__ float sigmoidf(float x) {
    return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x));
}

__global__ void preprocess_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ pair_repr,
    const float* __restrict__ pair_mask,
    const float* __restrict__ ln_w,
    const float* __restrict__ ln_b,
    const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w,
    const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b,
    const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w,
    const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b,
    float* __restrict__ a_out,
    float* __restrict__ b_out,
    int B, int N, int P, int H
) {
    __shared__ float warp_sums[2];
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* row = smem;
    
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    int n1 = blockIdx.y;
    int n2 = blockIdx.z;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    
    if (tid < P) {
        row[tid] = pair_repr[((b * N + n1) * N + n2) * P + tid];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    float x = (tid < P) ? row[tid] : 0.0f;
    float sum = x;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
        sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);
    }
    if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum;
    __syncthreads();
    
    float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1];
    float mean = total / P;
    float diff = (tid < P) ? (row[tid] - mean) : 0.0f;
    
    float sq = diff * diff;
    sum = sq;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
        sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);
    }
    if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum;
    __syncthreads();
    
    float var = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]) / P;
    float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);
    
    if (tid < P) {
        row[tid] = diff * inv_std * ln_w[tid] + ln_b[tid];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    if (tid < H) {
        int h = tid;
        float a_proj = proj_a_b[h];
        float a_gate = gate_a_b[h];
        float b_proj = proj_b_b[h];
        float b_gate = gate_b_b[h];
        
        #pragma unroll
        for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) {
            float xv = row[d];
            a_proj += xv * proj_a_w[h * P + d];
            a_gate += xv * gate_a_w[h * P + d];
            b_proj += xv * proj_b_w[h * P + d];
            b_gate += xv * gate_b_w[h * P + d];
        }
        
        float mask_val = pair_mask[(b * N + n1) * N + n2];
        a_out[((b * H + h) * N + n1) * N + n2] = a_proj * sigmoidf(a_gate) * mask_val;
        b_out[((b * H + h) * N + n1) * N + n2] = b_proj * sigmoidf(b_gate) * mask_val;
    }
}

__global__ void postprocess_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ einsum_out,
    const float* __restrict__ pair_repr,
    const float* __restrict__ ln_w,
    const float* __restrict__ ln_b,
    const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w,
    const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b,
    const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w,
    const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int B, int N, int H, int P
) {
    __shared__ float warp_sums[2];
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* einsum_row = smem;
    float* pair_row = einsum_row + H;
    
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    int i = blockIdx.y;
    int j = blockIdx.z;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    
    if (tid < H) {
        einsum_row[tid] = einsum_out[((b * H + tid) * N + i) * N + j];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    float x = (tid < H) ? einsum_row[tid] : 0.0f;
    float sum = x;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
        sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);
    }
    if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum;
    __syncthreads();
    
    float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1];
    float mean = total / H;
    float diff = (tid < H) ? (einsum_row[tid] - mean) : 0.0f;
    
    float sq = diff * diff;
    sum = sq;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
        sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);
    }
    if ((tid & 31) == 0) warp_sums[tid >> 5] = sum;
    __syncthreads();
    
    float var = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1]) / H;
    float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);
    
    if (tid < H) {
        einsum_row[tid] = diff * inv_std * ln_w[tid] + ln_b[tid];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    if (tid < P) {
        pair_row[tid] = pair_repr[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + tid];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    if (tid < P) {
        float out_val = out_proj_b[tid];
        for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) {
            out_val += einsum_row[h] * out_proj_w[tid * H + h];
        }
        
        float gate = out_gate_b[tid];
        for (int d = 0; d < P; d++) {
            gate += pair_row[d] * out_gate_w[tid * P + d];
        }
        
        output[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + tid] = sigmoidf(gate) * out_val;
    }
}

torch::Tensor forward_cuda(
    torch::Tensor pair_repr,
    torch::Tensor pair_mask,
    torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b,
    torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b,
    torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b,
    torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b,
    torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b,
    torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b,
    torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b,
    torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b
) {
    int B = pair_repr.size(0);
    int N = pair_repr.size(1);
    int P = pair_repr.size(3);
    int H = proj_a_w.size(0);
    
    auto options = pair_repr.options();
    auto a = torch::empty({B, H, N, N}, options);
    auto b = torch::empty({B, H, N, N}, options);
    
    dim3 grid(B, N, N);
    size_t smem_pre = P * sizeof(float);
    preprocess_kernel<<<grid, 64, smem_pre>>>(
        pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(),
        pair_mask.data_ptr<float>(),
        ln_in_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        proj_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        gate_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        proj_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        gate_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        a.data_ptr<float>(), b.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, N, P, H
    );
    
    auto a_r = a.reshape({B * H, N, N});
    auto b_r = b.reshape({B * H, N, N});
    auto c = torch::bmm(a_r.transpose(-2, -1), b_r);
    auto einsum = c.reshape({B, H, N, N});
    
    auto output = torch::empty_like(pair_repr);
    size_t smem_post = (H + P) * sizeof(float);
    postprocess_kernel<<<grid, 64, smem_post>>>(
        einsum.data_ptr<float>(),
        pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(),
        ln_out_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_proj_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_gate_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        output.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, N, H, P
    );
    
    return output;
}
"""

fused = load_inline(
    name="fused_tri_mult",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["forward_cuda"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden_dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.layer_norm_in = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.proj_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.layer_norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden_dim)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim)
        self.out_gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, pair_repr, pair_mask):
        pair_repr = pair_repr.contiguous()
        pair_mask = pair_mask.contiguous()
        return fused.forward_cuda(
            pair_repr, pair_mask,
            self.layer_norm_in.weight, self.layer_norm_in.bias,
            self.proj_a.weight, self.proj_a.bias,
            self.gate_a.weight, self.gate_a.bias,
            self.proj_b.weight, self.proj_b.bias,
            self.gate_b.weight, self.gate_b.bias,
            self.layer_norm_out.weight, self.layer_norm_out.bias,
            self.out_proj.weight, self.out_proj.bias,
            self.out_gate.weight, self.out_gate.bias,
        )
